style="font-size:105%;padding:0.2em 0 0.4em;text-align:center;padding-top:0.35em;">\n           <div class="hlist">\n            <ul>\n             <li>\n              <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">\n               Galileo\n              </a>\n             </li>\n             <li>\n              <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">\n               Newton\n              </a>\n             </li>\n             <li>\n              <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">\n               Kepler\n              </a>\n             </li>\n             <li>\n              <a href="/wiki/Jeremiah_Horrocks" title="Jeremiah Horrocks">\n               Horrocks\n              </a>\n             </li>\n             <li>\n              <a d\'Alembert\n              </a>\n             </li>\n             <li>\n              <a href="/wiki/Alexis_Clairaut" title="Alexis Clairaut">\n               Clairaut\n              </a>\n             </li>\n             <li>\n              <a href="/wiki/Joseph-Louis_Lagrange" title="Joseph-Louis Lagrange">\n               Lagrange\n              </a>\n             </li>\n             <li>\n              <a href="/wiki/Pierre-Simon_Laplace" title="Pierre-Simon Laplace">\n               Laplace\n              </a>\n             </li>\n             <li>\n              <a style="text-align:right;font-size:115%;padding-top: 0.6em;padding-top:0.15em;">\n         <div class="plainlinks hlist navbar mini">\n          <ul>\n           <li class="nv-view">\n            <a href="/wiki/Template:Classical_mechanics" title="Template:Classical mechanics">\n             <abbr title="View this template">\n              v\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n           <li class="nv-talk">\n            <a href="/wiki/Template_talk:Classical_mechanics" title="Template talk:Classical mechanics">\n             <abbr title="Discuss this template">\n              t\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n           <li class="nv-edit">\n            <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Template:Classical_mechanics&amp;action=edit">\n             <abbr title="Edit this template">\n              e\n             </abbr>\n            </a>\n           </li>\n          </ul>\n         </div>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       <b>\n        Mass\n       </b>\n       is both a\n       <a href="/wiki/Physical_property" title="Physical property">\n        property\n       </a>\n       of a\n       <a href="/wiki/Physical_body" title="Physical body">\n        physical body\n       </a>\n       and a\n       <a href="/wiki/Measure_(mathematics)" title="Measure (mathematics)">\n        measure\n       </a>\n       of its\n       <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">\n        resistance\n       </a>\n       to\n       <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">\n        acceleration\n       </a>\n       (a change in its state of\n       <a href="/wiki/Motion_(physics)" title="Motion (physics)">\n        motion\n       </a>\n       ) when a\n       <a href="/wiki/Net_force" title="Net force">\n        net force\n       </a>\n       is applied.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-1">\n         [1]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       It also determines the\n       <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">\n        strength\n       </a>\n       of its mutual\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitation" title="Gravitation">\n        gravitational\n       </a>\n       attraction to other bodies. The basic\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_unit" title="SI unit">\n        SI unit\n       </a>\n       of mass is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">\n        kilogram\n       </a>\n       (kg).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In\n       <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">\n        physics\n       </a>\n       , mass is not the same as\n       <a href="/wiki/Weight" title="Weight">\n        weight\n       </a>\n       , even though mass is often determined by measuring the object\'s weight using a\n       <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale#Spring_scales" title="Weighing scale">\n        spring scale\n       </a>\n       , rather than\n       <a href="/wiki/Weighing_scale#Balance_scales" title="Weighing scale">\n        balance scale\n       </a>\n       comparing it directly with known masses. An object on the Moon would weigh less than it does on Earth because of the lower gravity, but it would still have the same mass. This is because weight is a force, while mass is the property that (along with gravity) determines the strength of this force.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtonian_physics" title="Newtonian physics">\n        Newtonian physics\n       </a>\n       , mass can be generalized as the amount of\n       <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">\n        matter\n       </a>\n       in an object. However, at very high speeds,\n       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">\n        special relativity\n       </a>\n       states that the\n       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">\n        kinetic energy\n       </a>\n       of its motion becomes a significant additional source of mass. Thus, any stationary body having mass has an\n       <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass\xe2\x80\x93energy equivalence">\n        equivalent\n       </a>\n       amount of energy, and all\n       <a href="/wiki/Forms_of_energy" title="Forms of energy">\n        forms of energy\n       </a>\n       resist acceleration by a force and have gravitational attraction. In\n       <a href="/wiki/Modern_physics" title="Modern physics">\n        modern physics\n       </a>\n       , matter is not a fundamental concept because its definition has proven elusive.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       There are several distinct phenomena which can be used to measure mass. Although some theorists have speculated that some of these phenomena could be independent of each other,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-2">\n         [2]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       current experiments have found no difference in results regardless of how it is measured:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        <i>\n         Inertial mass\n        </i>\n        measures an object\'s resistance to being accelerated by a force (represented by the relationship\n        <span class="nowrap">\n         <i>\n          F\n         </i>\n         =\n         <i>\n          ma\n         </i>\n        </span>\n        ).\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <i>\n         Active gravitational mass\n        </i>\n        measures the gravitational force exerted by an object.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        <i>\n         Passive gravitational mass\n        </i>\n        measures the gravitational force exerted on an object in a known gravitational field.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       The mass of an object determines its acceleration in the presence of an applied force. The inertia and the inertial mass describe the same properties of physical bodies at the qualitative and quantitative level respectively, by other words, the mass quantitatively describes the inertia. According to\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_second_law_of_motion" title="Newton\'s second law of motion">\n        Newton\'s second law of motion\n       </a>\n       , if a body of fixed mass\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       is subjected to a single force\n       <i>\n        F\n       </i>\n       , its acceleration\n       <i>\n        a\n       </i>\n       is given by\n       <i>\n        F\n       </i>\n       /\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       . A body\'s mass also determines the degree to which it generates or is affected by a\n       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">\n        gravitational field\n       </a>\n       . If a first body of mass\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        A\n       </sub>\n       is placed at a distance\n       <i>\n        r\n       </i>\n       (center of mass to center of mass) from a second body of mass\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        B\n       </sub>\n       , each body is subject to an attractive force\n       <span class="nowrap">\n        <i>\n         F\n        </i>\n        <sub>\n         g\n        </sub>\n        =\n        <i>\n         Gm\n        </i>\n        <sub>\n         A\n        </sub>\n        <i>\n         m\n        </i>\n        <sub>\n         B\n        </sub>\n        /\n        <i>\n         r\n        </i>\n        <sup>\n         2\n        </sup>\n       </span>\n       , where\n       <span class="nowrap">\n        <i>\n         G\n        </i>\n        =\n        <span class="nowrap">\n         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">\n          6989667000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0\n         </span>\n         6.67\n         <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">\n          \xc3\x97\n         </span>\n         10\n         <sup>\n          \xe2\x88\x9211\n         </sup>\n         N kg\n         <sup>\n          \xe2\x88\x922\n         </sup>\n         m\n         <sup>\n          2\n         </sup>\n        </span>\n       </span>\n       is the "universal\n       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">\n        gravitational constant\n       </a>\n       ". This is sometimes referred to as gravitational mass.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-3">\n         [note 1]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Repeated experiments since the 17th century have demonstrated that inertial and gravitational mass are identical; since 1915, this observation has been entailed\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/A_priori_and_a_posteriori" title="A priori and a posteriori">\n         a priori\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">\n        equivalence principle\n       </a>\n       of\n       <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">\n        general relativity\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <div class="toc" id="toc">\n       <div class="toctitle">\n        <h2>\n         Contents\n        </h2>\n       </div>\n       <ul>\n        <li class="toclevel-1        </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14">\n         <a href="#Atomic_mass">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n      <ul>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-16">\n           <a href="#Special_relativity">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             6.1\n            </span>\n            <span particles and imaginary (complex) mass\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n          <li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20">\n           <a href="#Exotic_matter_and_negative_mass">\n            <span class="tocnumber">\n             7.2\n            </span>\n            <span class="toctext">\n             Exotic matter and negative mass\n            </span>\n           </a>\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-21">\n         <a href="#See_also">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           8\n          </span>\n class="tocnumber">\n           9\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           Notes\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23">\n         <a href="#References">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           10\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           References\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n        <li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24">\n         <a href="#External_links">\n          <span class="tocnumber">\n           11\n          </span>\n          <span class="toctext">\n           External links\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </li>\n       </ul>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Units_of_mass">\n        Units of mass\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Units of mass">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Further information:\n       <a href="/wiki/Orders_of_magnitude_(mass)" title="Orders of magnitude (mass)">\n        Orders of magnitude (mass)\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:SI_base_unit.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="600" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/SI_base_unit.svg/220px-SI_base_unit.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/SI_base_unit.svg/330px-SI_base_unit.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c8/SI_base_unit.svg/440px-SI_base_unit.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:SI_base_unit.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The kilogram is one of the seven\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/SI_base_units" title="SI base units">\n          SI base units\n         </a>\n         and one of three which is defined\n         <i>\n          ad hoc\n         </i>\n         (i.e. without reference to another base unit).\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The standard\n       <a href="/wiki/International_System_of_Units" title="International System of Units">\n        International System of Units\n       </a>\n       (SI) unit of mass is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Kilogram" title="Kilogram">\n        kilogram\n       </a>\n       (kg). The kilogram is 1000\xc2\xa0grams (g), first defined in 1795 as one cubic decimeter of water at the\n       <a href="/wiki/Melting_point" title="Melting point">\n        melting point\n       </a>\n       of ice. Then in 1889, the kilogram was redefined as the mass of the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/International_prototype_kilogram" title="International prototype kilogram">\n        international prototype kilogram\n       </a>\n       , and as such is independent of the meter, or the properties of water. However, the mass of the international prototype and its identical national copies have been found to be drifting over time. It is expected that the\n       <a href="/wiki/Proposed_redefinition_of_SI_base_units" title="Proposed redefinition of SI base units">\n        re-definition of the kilogram and several other units will change\n       </a>\n       on May 20, 2019, following a final vote by the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/CGPM" title="CGPM">\n        CGPM\n       </a>\n       in November 2018.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4">\n        <a href="#cite_note-4">\n         [3]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The new definition will use only invariant quantities of nature: the\n       <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">\n        speed of light\n       </a>\n       , the\n       <a href="/wiki/Caesium_standard" title="Caesium standard">\n        caesium hyperfine frequency\n       </a>\n       , and the\n       <a href="/wiki/Planck_constant" title="Planck constant">\n        Planck constant\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Brochure9_2016_5-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Brochure9_2016-5">\n         [4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Other units are accepted for use in SI:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        the\n        <a href="/wiki/Tonne" title="Tonne">\n         tonne\n        </a>\n        (t) (or "metric ton") is equal to 1000\xc2\xa0kg.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        the\n        <a href="/wiki/Electronvolt#Mass" title="Electronvolt">\n         electronvolt\n        </a>\n        (eV) is a unit of\n        <a href="/wiki/Energy" title="Energy">\n         energy\n        </a>\n        , but because of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass\xe2\x80\x93energy equivalence">\n         mass\xe2\x80\x93energy equivalence\n        </a>\n        it can easily be converted to a unit of mass, and is often used like one. In this context, the mass has units of eV/\n        <i>\n         c\n        </i>\n        <sup>\n         2\n        </sup>\n        (where\n        <i>\n         c\n        </i>\n        is the speed of light). The electronvolt and its multiples, such as the MeV (megaelectronvolt), are commonly used in\n        <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">\n         particle physics\n        </a>\n        .\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        the\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">\n         atomic mass unit\n        </a>\n        (u) is 1/12 of the mass of a\n        <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">\n         carbon-12\n        </a>\n        atom, approximately\n        <span class="nowrap">\n         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">\n          6973166000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0\n         </span>\n         1.66\n         <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">\n          \xc3\x97\n         </span>\n         10\n         <sup>\n          \xe2\x88\x9227\n         </sup>\n         kg\n        </span>\n        .\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6">\n         <a href="#cite_note-6">\n          [note 2]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        The atomic mass unit is convenient for expressing the masses of atoms and molecules.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <p>\n       Outside the SI system, other units of mass include:\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        the\n        <a href="/wiki/Slug_(unit)" title="Slug (unit)">\n         slug\n        </a>\n        (sl) is an\n        <a href="/wiki/Imperial_units" title="Imperial units">\n         Imperial unit\n        </a>\n        of mass (about 14.6\xc2\xa0kg).\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        the\n        <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">\n         pound\n        </a>\n        (lb) is a unit of both mass and force, used mainly in the United States (about 0.45\xc2\xa0kg or 4.5\xc2\xa0N). In scientific contexts where\n        <a href="/wiki/Pound_(force)" title="Pound (force)">\n         pound (force)\n        </a>\n        and\n        <a href="/wiki/Pound_(mass)" title="Pound (mass)">\n         pound (mass)\n        </a>\n        need to be distinguished, SI units are usually used instead.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        the\n        <a href="/wiki/Planck_mass" title="Planck mass">\n         Planck mass\n        </a>\n        (\n        <i>\n         m\n        </i>\n        <sub>\n         P\n        </sub>\n        ) is the maximum mass of point particles (about\n        <span class="nowrap">\n         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">\n          6992218000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0\n         </span>\n         2.18\n         <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">\n          \xc3\x97\n         </span>\n         10\n         <sup>\n          \xe2\x88\x928\n         </sup>\n         kg\n        </span>\n        ). It is used in\n        <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">\n         particle physics\n        </a>\n        .\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        the\n        <a href="/wiki/Solar_mass" title="Solar mass">\n         solar mass\n        </a>\n        (M\n        <sub>\n         \xe2\x98\x89\n        </sub>\n        ) is defined as the mass of the\n        <a href="/wiki/Sun" title="Sun">\n         Sun\n        </a>\n        . It is primarily used in astronomy to compare large masses such as stars or galaxies (\xe2\x89\x88\n        <span class="nowrap">\n         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">\n          7030199000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0\n         </span>\n         1.99\n         <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">\n          \xc3\x97\n         </span>\n         10\n         <sup>\n          30\n         </sup>\n         kg\n        </span>\n        ).\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        the mass of a very small particle may be identified by its inverse\n        <a href="/wiki/Compton_wavelength" title="Compton wavelength">\n         Compton wavelength\n        </a>\n        (\n        <span class="nowrap">\n         1\xc2\xa0cm\n         <sup>\n          \xe2\x88\x921\n         </sup>\n         \xe2\x89\x88\n         <span class="nowrap">\n          <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">\n           6959352000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0\n          </span>\n          3.52\n          <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">\n           \xc3\x97\n          </span>\n          10\n          <sup>\n           \xe2\x88\x9241\n          </sup>\n          kg\n         </span>\n        </span>\n        ).\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        the mass of a very large star or\n        <a href="/wiki/Black_hole" title="Black hole">\n         black hole\n        </a>\n        may be identified with its\n        <a href="/wiki/Schwarzschild_radius" title="Schwarzschild radius">\n         Schwarzschild radius\n        </a>\n        (\n        <span class="nowrap">\n         1\xc2\xa0cm \xe2\x89\x88\n         <span class="nowrap">\n          <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">\n           7024673000000000000\xe2\x99\xa0\n          </span>\n          6.73\n          <span style="margin-left:0.25em;margin-right:0.15em;">\n           \xc3\x97\n          </span>\n          10\n          <sup>\n           24\n          </sup>\n          kg\n         </span>\n        </span>\n        ).\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Definitions_of_mass">\n        Definitions of mass\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Definitions of mass">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:322px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:MassProperties.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="550" data-file-width="500" height="352" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/MassProperties.svg/320px-MassProperties.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/MassProperties.svg/480px-MassProperties.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/MassProperties.svg/640px-MassProperties.svg.png 2x" width="320"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:MassProperties.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         The relation between properties of mass and their associated physical constants. Every massive object is believed to exhibit all five properties. However, due to extremely large or extremely small constants, it is generally impossible to verify more than two or three properties for any object.\n         <ul>\n          <li>\n           The\n           <a href="/wiki/Schwarzschild_radius" title="Schwarzschild radius">\n            Schwarzschild radius\n           </a>\n           (\n           <span class="texhtml">\n            <i>\n             r\n            </i>\n            <sub>\n             s\n            </sub>\n           </span>\n           ) represents the ability of mass to cause curvature in space and time.\n          </li>\n          <li>\n           The\n           <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravitational_parameter" title="Standard gravitational parameter">\n            standard gravitational parameter\n           </a>\n           (\n           <span class="texhtml">\n            <i>\n             \xce\xbc\n            </i>\n           </span>\n           ) represents the ability of a massive body to exert Newtonian gravitational forces on other bodies.\n          </li>\n          <li>\n           Inertial\n           <a class="mw-selflink selflink">\n            mass\n           </a>\n           (\n           <span class="texhtml">\n            <i>\n             m\n            </i>\n           </span>\n           ) represents the Newtonian response of mass to forces.\n          </li>\n          <li>\n           <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass\xe2\x80\x93energy equivalence">\n            Rest energy\n           </a>\n           (\n           <span class="texhtml">\n            <i>\n             E\n            </i>\n            <sub>\n             0\n            </sub>\n           </span>\n           ) represents the ability of mass to be converted into other forms of energy.\n          </li>\n          <li>\n           The\n           <a href="/wiki/Compton_wavelength" title="Compton wavelength">\n            Compton wavelength\n           </a>\n           (\n           <span class="texhtml">\n            \xce\xbb\n           </span>\n           ) represents the quantum response of mass to local geometry.\n          </li>\n         </ul>\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Physical_science" title="Physical science">\n        physical science\n       </a>\n       , one may distinguish conceptually between at least seven different aspects of\n       <i>\n        mass\n       </i>\n       , or seven physical notions that involve the concept of\n       <i>\n        mass\n       </i>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rindler2_7-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Rindler2-7">\n         [5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Every experiment to date has shown these seven values to be\n       <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">\n        proportional\n       </a>\n       , and in some cases equal, and this proportionality gives rise to the abstract concept of mass. There are a number of ways mass can be measured or\n       <a href="/wiki/Operationalization" title="Operationalization">\n        operationally defined\n       </a>\n       :\n      </p>\n      <ul>\n       <li>\n        Inertial mass is a measure of an object\'s resistance to acceleration when a\n        <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">\n         force\n        </a>\n        is applied. It is determined by applying a force to an object and measuring the acceleration that results from that force. An object with small inertial mass will accelerate more than an object with large inertial mass when acted upon by the same force. One says the body of greater mass has greater\n        <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">\n         inertia\n        </a>\n        .\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Active gravitational mass\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8">\n         <a href="#cite_note-8">\n          [note 3]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n        is a measure of the strength of an object\'s\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gauss%27_law_for_gravity" title="Gauss\' law for gravity">\n         gravitational flux\n        </a>\n        (gravitational flux is equal to the surface integral of gravitational field over an enclosing surface). Gravitational field can be measured by allowing a small "test object" to fall freely and measuring its\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Free-fall" title="Free-fall">\n         free-fall\n        </a>\n        acceleration. For example, an object in free fall near the\n        <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">\n         Moon\n        </a>\n        is subject to a smaller gravitational field, and hence accelerates more slowly, than the same object would if it were in free fall near the Earth. The gravitational field near the Moon is weaker because the Moon has less active gravitational mass.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Passive gravitational mass is a measure of the strength of an object\'s interaction with a\n        <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_field" title="Gravitational field">\n         gravitational field\n        </a>\n        . Passive gravitational mass is determined by dividing an object\'s weight by its free-fall acceleration. Two objects within the same gravitational field will experience the same acceleration; however, the object with a smaller passive gravitational mass will experience a smaller force (less weight) than the object with a larger passive gravitational mass.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Energy also has mass according to the principle of\n        <a href="/wiki/Mass%E2%80%93energy_equivalence" title="Mass\xe2\x80\x93energy equivalence">\n         mass\xe2\x80\x93energy equivalence\n        </a>\n        . This equivalence is exemplified in a large number of physical processes including\n        <a href="/wiki/Pair_production" title="Pair production">\n         pair production\n        </a>\n        ,\n        <a href="/wiki/Nuclear_fusion" title="Nuclear fusion">\n         nuclear fusion\n        </a>\n        , and the gravitational\n        <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_lens" title="Gravitational lens">\n         bending of light\n        </a>\n        . Pair production and nuclear fusion are processes in which measurable amounts of mass are converted to energy, or vice versa. In the gravitational bending of light, photons of pure energy are shown to exhibit a behavior similar to passive gravitational mass.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Curvature of\n        <a href="/wiki/Spacetime" title="Spacetime">\n         spacetime\n        </a>\n        is a relativistic manifestation of the existence of mass. Such\n        <a href="/wiki/Curvature" title="Curvature">\n         curvature\n        </a>\n        is extremely weak and difficult to measure. For this reason, curvature was not discovered until after it was predicted by Einstein\'s theory of general relativity. Extremely precise\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_clocks" title="Atomic clocks">\n         atomic clocks\n        </a>\n        on the surface of the Earth, for example, are found to measure less time (run slower) when compared to similar clocks in space. This difference in elapsed time is a form of curvature called\n        <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_time_dilation" title="Gravitational time dilation">\n         gravitational time dilation\n        </a>\n        . Other forms of curvature have been measured using the\n        <a href="/wiki/Gravity_Probe_B" title="Gravity Probe B">\n         Gravity Probe B\n        </a>\n        satellite.\n       </li>\n       <li>\n        Quantum mass manifests itself as a difference between an object\'s quantum\n        <a href="/wiki/Frequency" title="Frequency">\n         frequency\n        </a>\n        and its\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wave_number" title="Wave number">\n         wave number\n        </a>\n        . The quantum mass of an electron, the\n        <a href="/wiki/Compton_wavelength" title="Compton wavelength">\n         Compton wavelength\n        </a>\n        , can be determined through various forms of\n        <a href="/wiki/Spectroscopy" title="Spectroscopy">\n         spectroscopy\n        </a>\n        and is closely related to the\n        <a href="/wiki/Rydberg_constant" title="Rydberg constant">\n         Rydberg constant\n        </a>\n        , the\n        <a href="/wiki/Bohr_radius" title="Bohr radius">\n         Bohr radius\n        </a>\n        , and the\n        <a href="/wiki/Classical_electron_radius" title="Classical electron radius">\n         classical electron radius\n        </a>\n        . The quantum mass of larger objects can be directly measured using a\n        <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Watt_balance" title="Watt balance">\n         Watt balance\n        </a>\n        . In relativistic quantum mechanics, mass is one of the irreducible representation labels of the Poincar\xc3\xa9 group.\n       </li>\n      </ul>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Weight_vs._mass">\n        Weight vs. mass\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Weight vs. mass">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight" title="Mass versus weight">\n        Mass versus weight\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In everyday usage, mass and "\n       <a href="/wiki/Weight" title="Weight">\n        weight\n       </a>\n       " are often used interchangeably. For instance, a person\'s weight may be stated as 75\xc2\xa0kg. In a constant gravitational field, the weight of an object is proportional to its mass, and it is unproblematic to use the same unit for both concepts. But because of slight differences in the strength of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Gravity_of_Earth" title="Gravity of Earth">\n        Earth\'s gravitational field\n       </a>\n       at different places, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Mass_versus_weight" title="Mass versus weight">\n        distinction\n       </a>\n       becomes important for measurements with a precision better than a few percent, and for places far from the surface of the Earth, such as in space or on other planets. Conceptually, "mass" (measured in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kilograms" title="Kilograms">\n        kilograms\n       </a>\n       ) refers to an intrinsic property of an object, whereas "weight" (measured in\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newtons" title="Newtons">\n        newtons\n       </a>\n       ) measures an object\'s resistance to deviating from its natural course of\n       <a href="/wiki/Free_fall" title="Free fall">\n        free fall\n       </a>\n       , which can be influenced by the nearby gravitational field. No matter how strong the gravitational field, objects in free fall are\n       <a href="/wiki/Weightlessness" title="Weightlessness">\n        weightless\n       </a>\n       , though they still have mass.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9">\n        <a href="#cite_note-9">\n         [6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The force known as "weight" is proportional to mass and\n       <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">\n        acceleration\n       </a>\n       in all situations where the mass is accelerated away from free fall. For example, when a body is at rest in a gravitational field (rather than in free fall), it must be accelerated by a force from a scale or the surface of a planetary body such as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">\n        Earth\n       </a>\n       or the\n       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">\n        Moon\n       </a>\n       . This force keeps the object from going into free fall. Weight is the opposing force in such circumstances, and is thus determined by the acceleration of free fall. On the surface of the Earth, for example, an object with a mass of 50\xc2\xa0kilograms weighs 491 newtons, which means that 491 newtons is being applied to keep the object from going into free fall. By contrast, on the surface of the Moon, the same object still has a mass of 50\xc2\xa0kilograms but weighs only 81.5\xc2\xa0newtons, because only 81.5 newtons is required to keep this object from going into a free fall on the moon. Restated in mathematical terms, on the surface of the Earth, the weight\n       <i>\n        W\n       </i>\n       of an object is related to its mass\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       by\n       <span class="nowrap">\n        <i>\n         W\n        </i>\n        =\n        <i>\n         mg\n        </i>\n       </span>\n       , where\n       <span class="nowrap">\n        <i>\n         g\n        </i>\n        =\n        <span class="nowrap">\n         <span class="sortkey" style="display:none">\n          7000980665000000000\xe2\x99\xa0\n         </span>\n         9.80665\xc2\xa0m/s\n         <sup>\n          2\n         </sup>\n        </span>\n       </span>\n       is the acceleration due to\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Earth%27s_gravity" title="Earth\'s gravity">\n        Earth\'s gravitational field\n       </a>\n       , (expressed as the acceleration experienced by a free-falling object).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       For other situations, such as when objects are subjected to mechanical accelerations from forces other than the resistance of a planetary surface, the weight force is proportional to the mass of an object multiplied by the total acceleration away from free fall, which is called the\n       <a href="/wiki/Proper_acceleration" title="Proper acceleration">\n        proper acceleration\n       </a>\n       . Through such mechanisms, objects in elevators, vehicles, centrifuges, and the like, may experience weight forces many times those caused by resistance to the effects of gravity on objects, resulting from planetary surfaces. In such cases, the generalized equation for weight\n       <i>\n        W\n       </i>\n       of an object is related to its mass\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       by the equation\n       <span class="nowrap">\n        <i>\n         W\n        </i>\n        = \xe2\x80\x93\n        <i>\n         ma\n        </i>\n       </span>\n       , where\n       <i>\n        a\n       </i>\n       is the proper acceleration of the object caused by all influences other than gravity. (Again, if gravity is the only influence, such as occurs when an object falls freely, its weight will be zero).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Macroscopically, mass is associated with\n       <a href="/wiki/Matter" title="Matter">\n        matter\n       </a>\n       , although matter is not, ultimately, as clearly defined a concept as mass. On the subatomic scale, not only\n       <a href="/wiki/Fermion" title="Fermion">\n        fermions\n       </a>\n       , the particles often associated with matter, but also some\n       <a href="/wiki/Boson" title="Boson">\n        bosons\n       </a>\n       , the particles that act as\n       <a href="/wiki/Force_carrier" title="Force carrier">\n        force carriers\n       </a>\n       , have\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">\n        rest mass\n       </a>\n       . Another problem for easy definition is that much of the rest mass of ordinary matter derives from the binding energy (potential energy) holding their quarks together and other forms of energy rather than the sum of the rest masses of the individual particle constituents. For example, only 1% of the rest mass of matter is accounted for by the rest mass of its elementary quarks and electrons. From a fundamental physics perspective, mass is the number describing under which the\n       <a href="/wiki/Representation_(mathematics)" title="Representation (mathematics)">\n        representation\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Little_group" title="Little group">\n        little group\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Poincar%C3%A9_group" title="Poincar\xc3\xa9 group">\n        Poincar\xc3\xa9 group\n       </a>\n       a particle transforms. In the\n       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">\n        Standard Model\n       </a>\n       of particle physics, this symmetry is described as arising as a consequence of a coupling of particles with rest mass to a postulated additional\n       <a href="/wiki/Field_(physics)" title="Field (physics)">\n        field\n       </a>\n       , known as the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Higgs_field" title="Higgs field">\n        Higgs field\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The total mass of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Observable_universe" title="Observable universe">\n        observable universe\n       </a>\n       is estimated at 10\n       <sup>\n        53\n       </sup>\n       kg,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10">\n        <a href="#cite_note-10">\n         [7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       corresponding to the rest mass of between 10\n       <sup>\n        79\n       </sup>\n       and 10\n       <sup>\n        80\n       </sup>\n       <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">\n        protons\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (January 2014)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_vs._gravitational_mass">\n        Inertial vs. gravitational mass\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Inertial vs. gravitational mass">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <p>\n       Although inertial mass, passive gravitational mass and active gravitational mass are conceptually distinct, no experiment has ever unambiguously demonstrated any difference between them. In\n       <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">\n        classical mechanics\n       </a>\n       , Newton\'s third law implies that active and passive gravitational mass must always be identical (or at least proportional), but the classical theory offers no compelling reason why the gravitational mass has to equal the inertial mass. That it does is merely an empirical fact.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Albert_Einstein" title="Albert Einstein">\n        Albert Einstein\n       </a>\n       developed his\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/General_theory_of_relativity" title="General theory of relativity">\n        general theory of relativity\n       </a>\n       starting from the assumption that this correspondence between inertial and (passive) gravitational mass is not accidental: that no experiment will ever detect a difference between them (the weak version of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Equivalence_principle" title="Equivalence principle">\n        equivalence principle\n       </a>\n       ). However, in the resulting theory, gravitation is not a force and thus not subject to Newton\'s third law, so "the equality of inertial and\n       <i>\n        active\n       </i>\n       gravitational mass [...] remains as puzzling as ever".\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Rindler3_11-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-Rindler3-11">\n         [8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The equivalence of inertial and gravitational masses is sometimes referred to as the "Galilean equivalence principle" or the "\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weak_equivalence_principle" title="Weak equivalence principle">\n        weak equivalence principle\n       </a>\n       ". The most important consequence of this equivalence principle applies to freely falling objects. Suppose we have an object with inertial and gravitational masses\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       and\n       <i>\n        M\n       </i>\n       , respectively. If the only force acting on the object comes from a gravitational field\n       <i>\n        g\n       </i>\n       , combining Newton\'s second law and the gravitational law yields the acceleration\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mi>\n               a\n              </mi>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <mi>\n                 M\n                </mi>\n                <mi>\n                 m\n                </mi>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mi>\n               g\n              </mi>\n              <mo>\n               .\n              </mo>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle a={\\frac {M}{m}}g.}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="a={\\frac {M}{m}}g." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/690354b09c7904b01ad029e17859732568978f01" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:9.422ex; height:5.343ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       This says that the ratio of gravitational to inertial mass of any object is equal to some constant\n       <i>\n        K\n       </i>\n       <a href="/wiki/If_and_only_if" title="If and only if">\n        if and only if\n       </a>\n       all objects fall at the same rate in a given gravitational field. This phenomenon is referred to as the "universality of free-fall". (In addition, the constant\n       <i>\n        K\n       </i>\n       can be taken to be 1 by defining our units appropriately.)\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The first experiments demonstrating the universality of free-fall were conducted by\n       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">\n        Galileo\n       </a>\n       . It is commonly stated that Galileo obtained his results by dropping objects from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa" title="Leaning Tower of Pisa">\n        Leaning Tower of Pisa\n       </a>\n       , but this is most likely apocryphal; actually, he performed his experiments with balls rolling down nearly frictionless\n       <a href="/wiki/Inclined_plane" title="Inclined plane">\n        inclined planes\n       </a>\n       to slow the motion and increase the timing accuracy. Increasingly precise experiments have been performed, such as those performed by\n       <a href="/wiki/Lor%C3%A1nd_E%C3%B6tv%C3%B6s" title="Lor\xc3\xa1nd E\xc3\xb6tv\xc3\xb6s">\n        Lor\xc3\xa1nd E\xc3\xb6tv\xc3\xb6s\n       </a>\n       ,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12">\n        <a href="#cite_note-12">\n         [9]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       using the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Torsion_balance" title="Torsion balance">\n        torsion balance\n       </a>\n       pendulum, in 1889. As of 2008\n       <sup class="plainlinks noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;">\n        <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit">\n         [update]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       , no deviation from universality, and thus from Galilean equivalence, has ever been found, at least to the precision 10\n       <sup>\n        \xe2\x88\x9212\n       </sup>\n       . More precise experimental efforts are still being carried out.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       The universality of free-fall only applies to systems in which gravity is the only acting force. All other forces, especially\n       <a href="/wiki/Friction" title="Friction">\n        friction\n       </a>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Air_resistance" title="Air resistance">\n        air resistance\n       </a>\n       , must be absent or at least negligible. For example, if a hammer and a feather are dropped from the same height through the air on Earth, the feather will take much longer to reach the ground; the feather is not really in\n       <i>\n        free\n       </i>\n       -fall because the force of air resistance upwards against the feather is comparable to the downward force of gravity. On the other hand, if the experiment is performed in a\n       <a href="/wiki/Vacuum" title="Vacuum">\n        vacuum\n       </a>\n       , in which there is no air resistance, the hammer and the feather should hit the ground at exactly the same time (assuming the acceleration of both objects towards each other, and of the ground towards both objects, for its own part, is negligible). This can easily be done in a high school laboratory by dropping the objects in transparent tubes that have the air removed with a vacuum pump. It is even more dramatic when done in an environment that naturally has a vacuum, as\n       <a href="/wiki/David_Scott" title="David Scott">\n        David Scott\n       </a>\n       did on the surface of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Moon" title="Moon">\n        Moon\n       </a>\n       during\n       <a href="/wiki/Apollo_15" title="Apollo 15">\n        Apollo 15\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A stronger version of the equivalence principle, known as the\n       <i>\n        Einstein equivalence principle\n       </i>\n       or the\n       <i>\n        strong equivalence principle\n       </i>\n       , lies at the heart of the\n       <a href="/wiki/General_relativity" title="General relativity">\n        general theory of relativity\n       </a>\n       . Einstein\'s equivalence principle states that within sufficiently small regions of space-time, it is impossible to distinguish between a uniform acceleration and a uniform gravitational field. Thus, the theory postulates that the force acting on a massive object caused by a gravitational field is a result of the object\'s tendency to move in a straight line (in other words its inertia) and should therefore be a function of its inertial mass and the strength of the gravitational field.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Origin_of_mass">\n        Origin of mass\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Origin of mass">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_generation_mechanism" title="Mass generation mechanism">\n        Mass generation mechanism\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In\n       <a href="/wiki/Theoretical_physics" title="Theoretical physics">\n        theoretical physics\n       </a>\n       , a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mass_generation_mechanism" title="Mass generation mechanism">\n        mass generation mechanism\n       </a>\n       is a theory which attempts to explain the origin of mass from the most fundamental laws of\n       <a href="/wiki/Physics" title="Physics">\n        physics\n       </a>\n       . To date, a number of different models have been proposed which advocate different views of the origin of mass. The problem is complicated by the fact that the notion of mass is strongly related to the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gravitational_interaction" title="Gravitational interaction">\n        gravitational interaction\n       </a>\n       but a theory of the latter has not been yet reconciled with the currently popular model of\n       <a href="/wiki/Particle_physics" title="Particle physics">\n        particle physics\n       </a>\n       , known as the\n       <a href="/wiki/Standard_Model" title="Standard Model">\n        Standard Model\n       </a>\n       .\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Pre-Newtonian_concepts">\n        Pre-Newtonian concepts\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Pre-Newtonian concepts">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Weight_as_an_amount">\n        Weight as an amount\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Weight as an amount">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Weight" title="Weight">\n        weight\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Weighing_of_the_heart3.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="865" data-file-width="1056" height="180" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Weighing_of_the_heart3.jpg/220px-Weighing_of_the_heart3.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Weighing_of_the_heart3.jpg/330px-Weighing_of_the_heart3.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/71/Weighing_of_the_heart3.jpg/440px-Weighing_of_the_heart3.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Weighing_of_the_heart3.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Depiction of early\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balance_scales" title="Balance scales">\n          balance scales\n         </a>\n         in the\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papyrus_of_Hunefer" title="Papyrus of Hunefer">\n          Papyrus of Hunefer\n         </a>\n         (dated to the\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nineteenth_dynasty_of_Egypt" title="Nineteenth dynasty of Egypt">\n          19th dynasty\n         </a>\n         , ca. 1285 BC). The scene shows\n         <a href="/wiki/Anubis" title="Anubis">\n          Anubis\n         </a>\n         weighing the heart of Hunefer.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       The concept of\n       <a class="extiw" href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/amount" title="wikt:amount">\n        amount\n       </a>\n       is very old and\n       <a href="/wiki/Prehistoric_numerals" title="Prehistoric numerals">\n        predates recorded history\n       </a>\n       . Humans, at some early era, realized that the weight of a collection of similar objects was\n       <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">\n        directly proportional\n       </a>\n       to the number of objects in the collection:\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <msub>\n               <mi>\n                W\n               </mi>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mi>\n                 n\n                </mi>\n               </mrow>\n              </msub>\n              <mo>\n               \xe2\x88\x9d\n               <!-- \xe2\x88\x9d -->\n              </mo>\n              <mi>\n               n\n              </mi>\n              <mo>\n               ,\n              </mo>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle W_{n}\\propto n,}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="W_{n}\\propto n," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/c94e84bf7829bbaddaca1654415ff064a290751e" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.601ex; height:2.509ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       where\n       <i>\n        W\n       </i>\n       is the weight of the collection of similar objects and\n       <i>\n        n\n       </i>\n       is the number of objects in the collection. Proportionality, by definition, implies that two values have a constant\n       <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">\n        ratio\n       </a>\n       :\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <msub>\n                 <mi>\n                  W\n                 </mi>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mi>\n                   n\n                  </mi>\n                 </mrow>\n                </msub>\n                <mi>\n                 n\n                </mi>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <msub>\n                 <mi>\n                  W\n                 </mi>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mi>\n                   m\n                  </mi>\n                 </mrow>\n                </msub>\n                <mi>\n                 m\n                </mi>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle {\\frac {W_{n}}{n}}={\\frac {W_{m}}{m}}}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="{\\frac {W_{n}}{n}}={\\frac {W_{m}}{m}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/366e5fad98810346603f2f748934b2b0c3ac61a4" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:12.098ex; height:5.343ex;"/>\n        </span>\n        , or equivalently\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <msub>\n                 <mi>\n                  W\n                 </mi>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mi>\n                   n\n                  </mi>\n                 </mrow>\n                </msub>\n                <msub>\n                 <mi>\n                  W\n                 </mi>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mi>\n                   m\n                  </mi>\n                 </mrow>\n                </msub>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <mi>\n                 n\n                </mi>\n                <mi>\n                 m\n                </mi>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               .\n              </mo>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle {\\frac {W_{n}}{W_{m}}}={\\frac {n}{m}}.}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="{\\frac {W_{n}}{W_{m}}}={\\frac {n}{m}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/21a231ca0a23556ccb2ca53659b2a37c634ff96e" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:11.376ex; height:5.843ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       An early use of this relationship is a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Balance_scale" title="Balance scale">\n        balance scale\n       </a>\n       , which balances the force of one object\'s weight against the force of another object\'s weight. The two sides of a balance scale are close enough that the objects experience similar gravitational fields. Hence, if they have similar masses then their weights will also be similar. This allows the scale, by comparing weights, to also compare masses.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Consequently, historical weight standards were often defined in terms of amounts. The Romans, for example, used the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carob" title="Carob">\n        carob\n       </a>\n       seed (\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carat_(unit)" title="Carat (unit)">\n        carat\n       </a>\n       or\n       <a href="/wiki/Siliqua" title="Siliqua">\n        siliqua\n       </a>\n       ) as a measurement standard. If an object\'s weight was equivalent to\n       <a class="external text" href="http://std.dkuug.dk/JTC1/SC2/WG2/docs/n3138.pdf" rel="nofollow">\n        1728 carob seeds\n       </a>\n       , then the object was said to weigh one Roman pound. If, on the other hand, the object\'s weight was equivalent to\n       <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_units_of_measurement" title="Ancient Roman units of measurement">\n        144 carob seeds\n       </a>\n       then the object was said to weigh one Roman ounce (uncia). The Roman pound and ounce were both defined in terms of different sized collections of the same common mass standard, the carob seed. The ratio of a Roman ounce (144 carob seeds) to a Roman pound (1728 carob seeds) was:\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  o\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  u\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  n\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  c\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  e\n                 </mi>\n                </mrow>\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  p\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  o\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  u\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  n\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  d\n                 </mi>\n                </mrow>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <msub>\n                 <mi>\n                  W\n                 </mi>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mn>\n                   144\n                  </mn>\n                 </mrow>\n                </msub>\n                <msub>\n                 <mi>\n                  W\n                 </mi>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mn>\n                   1728\n                  </mn>\n                 </mrow>\n                </msub>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <mn>\n                 144\n                </mn>\n                <mn>\n                 1728\n                </mn>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n
