 <mo>\n               .\n              </mo>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle {\\frac {\\mathrm {ounce} }{\\mathrm {pound} }}={\\frac {W_{144}}{W_{1728}}}={\\frac {144}{1728}}={\\frac {1}{12}}.}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="{\\frac {\\mathrm {ounce} }{\\mathrm {pound} }}={\\frac {W_{144}}{W_{1728}}}={\\frac {144}{1728}}={\\frac {1}{12}}." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/4b63bc025c2c5a45ce9c9a0b74a1fc158a9e7748" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:32.505ex; height:5.843ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Planetary_motion">\n        Planetary motion\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Planetary motion">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       See also:\n       <a href="/wiki/Kepler%27s_laws_of_planetary_motion" title="Kepler\'s laws of planetary motion">\n        Kepler\'s laws of planetary motion\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In 1600 AD,\n       <a href="/wiki/Johannes_Kepler" title="Johannes Kepler">\n        Johannes Kepler\n       </a>\n       sought employment with\n       <a href="/wiki/Tycho_Brahe" title="Tycho Brahe">\n        Tycho Brahe\n       </a>\n       , who had some of the most precise astronomical data available. Using Brahe\'s precise observations of the planet Mars, Kepler spent the next five years developing his own method for characterizing planetary motion. In 1609, Johannes Kepler published his three laws of planetary motion, explaining how the planets orbit the Sun. In Kepler\'s final planetary model, he described planetary orbits as following\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Elliptical" title="Elliptical">\n        elliptical\n       </a>\n       paths with the Sun at a focal point of the ellipse. Kepler discovered that the\n       <a href="/wiki/Square_(algebra)" title="Square (algebra)">\n        square\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Orbital_period" title="Orbital period">\n        orbital period\n       </a>\n       of each planet is directly\n       <a href="/wiki/Proportionality_(mathematics)" title="Proportionality (mathematics)">\n        proportional\n       </a>\n       to the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cube_(arithmetic)" title="Cube (arithmetic)">\n        cube\n       </a>\n       of the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">\n        semi-major axis\n       </a>\n       of its orbit, or equivalently, that the\n       <a href="/wiki/Ratio" title="Ratio">\n        ratio\n       </a>\n       of these two values is constant for all planets in the\n       <a href="/wiki/Solar_System" title="Solar System">\n        Solar System\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13">\n        <a href="#cite_note-13">\n         [note 4]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       On 25 August 1609,\n       <a href="/wiki/Galileo_Galilei" title="Galileo Galilei">\n        Galileo Galilei\n       </a>\n       demonstrated his first telescope to a group of Venetian merchants, and in early January 1610, Galileo observed four dim objects near Jupiter, which he mistook for stars. However, after a few days of observation, Galileo realized that these "stars" were in fact orbiting Jupiter. These four objects (later named the\n       <a href="/wiki/Galilean_moons" title="Galilean moons">\n        Galilean moons\n       </a>\n       in honor of their discoverer) were the first celestial bodies observed to orbit something other than the Earth or Sun. Galileo continued to observe these moons over the next eighteen months, and by the middle of 1611 he had obtained remarkably accurate estimates for their periods.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Galilean_free_fall">\n        Galilean free fall\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Galilean free fall">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="405" data-file-width="330" height="209" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/170px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg/255px-Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/c/cc/Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Galileo.arp.300pix.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Galileo Galilei (1636)\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Falling_ball.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2751" data-file-width="819" height="571" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Falling_ball.jpg/170px-Falling_ball.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Falling_ball.jpg/255px-Falling_ball.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/02/Falling_ball.jpg/340px-Falling_ball.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Falling_ball.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Distance traveled by a freely falling ball is proportional to the square of the elapsed time\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Sometime prior to 1638, Galileo turned his attention to the phenomenon of objects in free fall, attempting to characterize these motions. Galileo was not the first to investigate Earth\'s gravitational field, nor was he the first to accurately describe its fundamental characteristics. However, Galileo\'s reliance on scientific experimentation to establish physical principles would have a profound effect on future generations of scientists. It is unclear if these were just hypothetical experiments used to illustrate a concept, or if they were real experiments performed by Galileo,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14">\n        <a href="#cite_note-14">\n         [10]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       but the results obtained from these experiments were both realistic and compelling. A biography by Galileo\'s pupil\n       <a href="/wiki/Vincenzo_Viviani" title="Vincenzo Viviani">\n        Vincenzo Viviani\n       </a>\n       stated that Galileo had dropped\n       <a href="/wiki/Ball" title="Ball">\n        balls\n       </a>\n       of the same material, but different masses, from the\n       <a href="/wiki/Leaning_Tower_of_Pisa" title="Leaning Tower of Pisa">\n        Leaning Tower of Pisa\n       </a>\n       to demonstrate that their time of descent was independent of their mass.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15">\n        <a href="#cite_note-15">\n         [note 5]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In support of this conclusion, Galileo had advanced the following theoretical argument: He asked if two bodies of different masses and different rates of fall are tied by a string, does the combined system fall faster because it is now more massive, or does the lighter body in its slower fall hold back the heavier body? The only convincing resolution to this question is that all bodies must fall at the same rate.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16">\n        <a href="#cite_note-16">\n         [11]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       A later experiment was described in Galileo\'s\n       <i>\n        Two New Sciences\n       </i>\n       published in 1638. One of Galileo\'s fictional characters, Salviati, describes an experiment using a bronze ball and a wooden ramp. The wooden ramp was "12 cubits long, half a cubit wide and three finger-breadths thick" with a straight, smooth, polished\n       <a href="/wiki/Groove_(engineering)" title="Groove (engineering)">\n        groove\n       </a>\n       . The groove was lined with "\n       <a href="/wiki/Parchment" title="Parchment">\n        parchment\n       </a>\n       , also smooth and polished as possible". And into this groove was placed "a hard, smooth and very round bronze ball". The ramp was inclined at various\n       <a href="/wiki/Angle" title="Angle">\n        angles\n       </a>\n       to slow the acceleration enough so that the elapsed time could be measured. The ball was allowed to roll a known distance down the ramp, and the time taken for the ball to move the known distance was measured. The time was measured using a water clock described as follows:\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        "a large vessel of water placed in an elevated position; to the bottom of this vessel was soldered a pipe of small diameter giving a thin jet of water, which we collected in a small glass during the time of each descent, whether for the whole length of the channel or for a part of its length; the water thus collected was weighed, after each descent, on a very accurate balance; the differences and ratios of these weights gave us the differences and ratios of the times, and this with such accuracy that although the operation was repeated many, many times, there was no appreciable discrepancy in the results."\n        <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17">\n         <a href="#cite_note-17">\n          [12]\n         </a>\n        </sup>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       Galileo found that for an object in free fall, the distance that the object has fallen is always proportional to the square of the elapsed time:\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mtext>\n                Distance\n               </mtext>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               \xe2\x88\x9d\n               <!-- \xe2\x88\x9d -->\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <msup>\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                 <mtext>\n                  Time\n                 </mtext>\n                </mrow>\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                 <mn>\n                  2\n                 </mn>\n                </mrow>\n               </msup>\n              </mrow>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle {\\text{Distance}}\\propto {{\\text{Time}}^{2}}}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="{\\text{Distance}}\\propto {{\\text{Time}}^{2}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/863dcd2eb117ebaf6929ce3a339486c07a4e05a8" style="vertical-align: -0.338ex; width:18.352ex; height:2.676ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       Galileo had shown that objects in free fall under the influence of the Earth\xe2\x80\x99s gravitational field have a constant acceleration, and Galileo\xe2\x80\x99s contemporary, Johannes Kepler, had shown that the planets follow elliptical paths under the influence of the Sun\xe2\x80\x99s gravitational mass. However, Galileo\xe2\x80\x99s free fall motions and Kepler\xe2\x80\x99s planetary motions remained distinct during Galileo\xe2\x80\x99s lifetime.\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Newtonian_mass">\n        Newtonian mass\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Newtonian mass">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1074" data-file-width="780" height="234" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/170px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/255px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/39/GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg/340px-GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg 2x" width="170"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:GodfreyKneller-IsaacNewton-1689.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Isaac Newton 1689\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <table cellspacing="2" class="wikitable" style="float:right;">\n       <tr>\n        <th colspan="2">\n         Earth\'s Moon\n        </th>\n        <th rowspan="2">\n         Mass of Earth\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Semi-major_axis" title="Semi-major axis">\n          Semi-major axis\n         </a>\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sidereal_orbital_period" title="Sidereal orbital period">\n          Sidereal orbital period\n         </a>\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         0.002 569\n         <a href="/wiki/Astronomical_unit" title="Astronomical unit">\n          AU\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         0.074 802\n         <a href="/wiki/Sidereal_year" title="Sidereal year">\n          sidereal year\n         </a>\n        </td>\n        <td rowspan="3">\n         <span class="mwe-math-element">\n          <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n           <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n <!-- \xe2\x88\x92 -->\n                 </mo>\n                 <mn>\n                  5\n                 </mn>\n                </mrow>\n               </msup>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mfrac>\n                 <msup>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mtext>\n                    AU\n                   </mtext>\n                  </mrow>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mn>\n                    3\n                   </mn>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </msup>\n                 <msup>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mtext>\n                    y\n                   </mtext>\n                  </mrow>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mn>\n                    2\n                   </mn>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </msup>\n                </mfrac>\n               </mrow>\n               <mo>\n                =\n               </mo>\n               <mn>\n                3.986\n               </mn>\n               <mo>\n                \xe2\x8b\x85\n             <mn>\n                  14\n                 </mn>\n                </mrow>\n               </msup>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mfrac>\n <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mtext>\n                    s\n                   </mtext>\n                  </mrow>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n {{\\text{m}}^{3}}{{\\text{s}}^{2}}}}\n             </annotation>\n            </semantics>\n           </math>\n          </span>\n          <img alt="1.2\\pi ^{2}\\cdot 10^{-5}{\\frac {{\\text{AU}}^{3}}{{\\text{y}}^{2}}}=3.986\\cdot 10^{14}{\\frac {{\\text{m}}^{3}}{{\\text{s}}^{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/17e92e090b678560982a121e5e261a117a7a1d8e" style="vertical-align: -2.505ex; width:35.426ex; height:6.509ex;"/>\n         </span>\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <th>\n         Earth\'s gravity\n        </th>\n        <th>\n         Earth\'s radius\n        </th>\n       </tr>\n       <tr>\n        <td>\n         9.806 65\xc2\xa0m/s\n         <sup>\n          2\n         </sup>\n        </td>\n        <td>\n         6 375\xc2\xa0km\n        </td>\n       </tr>\n      </table>\n      <p>\n       <a href="/wiki/Robert_Hooke" title="Robert Hooke">\n        Robert Hooke\n       </a>\n       had published his concept of gravitational forces in 1674, stating that all\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Celestial_bodies" title="Celestial bodies">\n        celestial bodies\n       </a>\n       have an attraction or gravitating power towards their own centers, and also attract all the other celestial bodies that are within the sphere of their activity. He further stated that gravitational attraction increases by how much nearer the body wrought upon is to their own center.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-18">\n        <a href="#cite_note-18">\n         [13]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       In correspondence with\n       <a href="/wiki/Isaac_Newton" title="Isaac Newton">\n        Isaac Newton\n       </a>\n       from 1679 and 1680, Hooke conjectured that gravitational forces might decrease according to the double of the distance between the two bodies.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-19">\n        <a href="#cite_note-19">\n         [14]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Hooke urged Newton, who was a pioneer in the development of\n       <a href="/wiki/Calculus" title="Calculus">\n        calculus\n       </a>\n       , to work through the mathematical details of Keplerian orbits to determine if Hooke\'s hypothesis was correct. Newton\'s own investigations verified that Hooke was correct, but due to personal differences between the two men, Newton chose not to reveal this to Hooke. Isaac Newton kept quiet about his discoveries until 1684, at which time he told a friend,\n       <a href="/wiki/Edmond_Halley" title="Edmond Halley">\n        Edmond Halley\n       </a>\n       , that he had solved the problem of gravitational orbits, but had misplaced the solution in his office.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20">\n        <a href="#cite_note-20">\n         [15]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       After being encouraged by Halley, Newton decided to develop his ideas about gravity and publish all of his findings. In November 1684, Isaac Newton sent a document to Edmund Halley, now lost but presumed to have been titled\n       <i>\n        <a href="/wiki/De_motu_corporum_in_gyrum" title="De motu corporum in gyrum">\n         De motu corporum in gyrum\n        </a>\n       </i>\n       (Latin for "On the motion of bodies in an orbit").\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21">\n        <a href="#cite_note-21">\n         [16]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       Halley presented Newton\'s findings to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Royal_Society" title="Royal Society">\n        Royal Society\n       </a>\n       of London, with a promise that a fuller presentation would follow. Newton later recorded his ideas in a three book set, entitled\n       <a href="/wiki/Philosophi%C3%A6_Naturalis_Principia_Mathematica" title="Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica">\n        Philosophi\xc3\xa6 Naturalis Principia Mathematica\n       </a>\n       (Latin: "Mathematical Principles of Natural Philosophy"). The first was received by the Royal Society on 28 April 1685\xe2\x80\x936; the second on 2 March 1686\xe2\x80\x937; and the third on 6 April 1686\xe2\x80\x937. The Royal Society published Newton\xe2\x80\x99s entire collection at their own expense in May 1686\xe2\x80\x937.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-principia_22-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-principia-22">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :31\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Isaac Newton had bridged the gap between Kepler\xe2\x80\x99s gravitational mass and Galileo\xe2\x80\x99s gravitational acceleration, resulting in the discovery of the following relationship which governed both of these:\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                g\n               </mi>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mo>\n               \xe2\x88\x92\n               <!-- \xe2\x88\x92 -->\n              </mo>\n              <mi>\n               \xce\xbc\n               <!-- \xce\xbc -->\n              </mi>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                 <mover>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                    R\n                   </mi>\n                  </mrow>\n                  <mo stretchy="false">\n                   ^\n                   <!-- ^ -->\n                  </mo>\n                 </mover>\n                </mrow>\n                <mrow>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mo stretchy="false">\n                   |\n                  </mo>\n                 </mrow>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                   R\n                  </mi>\n                 </mrow>\n                 <msup>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mo stretchy="false">\n                    |\n                   </mo>\n                  </mrow>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mn>\n                    2\n                   </mn>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </msup>\n                </mrow>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle \\mathbf {g} =-\\mu {\\frac {\\hat {\\mathbf {R} }}{|\\mathbf {R} |^{2}}}}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="\\mathbf {g} =-\\mu {\\frac {\\hat {\\mathbf {R} }}{|\\mathbf {R} |^{2}}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/efeea88dd410e8cd05639b4c2b62a4d238a9c6e1" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:12.913ex; height:7.343ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       where\n       <b>\n        g\n       </b>\n       is the apparent acceleration of a body as it passes through a region of space where gravitational fields exist,\n       <i>\n        \xce\xbc\n       </i>\n       is the gravitational mass (\n       <a href="/wiki/Standard_gravitational_parameter" title="Standard gravitational parameter">\n        standard gravitational parameter\n       </a>\n       ) of the body causing gravitational fields, and\n       <b>\n        R\n       </b>\n       is the radial coordinate (the distance between the centers of the two bodies).\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       By finding the exact relationship between a body\'s gravitational mass and its gravitational field, Newton provided a second method for measuring gravitational mass. The mass of the Earth can be determined using Kepler\'s method (from the orbit of Earth\'s Moon), or it can be determined by measuring the gravitational acceleration on the Earth\'s surface, and multiplying that by the square of the Earth\'s radius. The mass of the Earth is approximately three millionths of the mass of the Sun. To date, no other accurate method for measuring gravitational mass has been discovered.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23">\n        <a href="#cite_note-23">\n         [18]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span id="Newton\'s_cannonball">\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Newton.27s_cannonball">\n        Newton\'s cannonball\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Newton\'s cannonball">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Newton_Cannon.svg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="240" data-file-width="240" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Newton_Cannon.svg/220px-Newton_Cannon.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Newton_Cannon.svg/330px-Newton_Cannon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/73/Newton_Cannon.svg/440px-Newton_Cannon.svg.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Newton_Cannon.svg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         A cannon on top of a very high mountain shoots a cannonball horizontally. If the speed is low, the cannonball quickly falls back to Earth (A,B). At\n         <a href="/wiki/Orbital_speed" title="Orbital speed">\n          intermediate speeds\n         </a>\n         , it will revolve around Earth along an elliptical orbit (C,D). At\n         <a href="/wiki/Escape_velocity" title="Escape velocity">\n          a sufficiently high speed\n         </a>\n         , it will leave the Earth altogether (E).\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_cannonball" title="Newton\'s cannonball">\n        Newton\'s cannonball\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Newton\'s cannonball was a\n       <a href="/wiki/Thought_experiment" title="Thought experiment">\n        thought experiment\n       </a>\n       used to bridge the gap between Galileo\'s gravitational acceleration and Kepler\'s elliptical orbits. It appeared in Newton\'s 1728 book\n       <i>\n        A Treatise of the System of the World\n       </i>\n       . According to Galileo\'s concept of gravitation, a dropped stone falls with constant acceleration down towards the Earth. However, Newton explains that when a stone is thrown horizontally (meaning sideways or perpendicular to Earth\'s gravity) it follows a curved path. "For a stone projected is by the pressure of its own weight forced out of the rectilinear path, which by the projection alone it should have pursued, and made to describe a curve line in the air; and through that crooked way is at last brought down to the ground. And the greater the velocity is with which it is projected, the farther it goes before it falls to the Earth."\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-principia_22-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-principia-22">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :513\n       </sup>\n       Newton further reasons that if an object were "projected in an horizontal direction from the top of a high mountain" with sufficient velocity, "it would reach at last quite beyond the circumference of the Earth, and return to the mountain from which it was projected."\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed">\n          <span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (December 2013)">\n           citation needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Universal_gravitational_mass">\n        Universal gravitational mass\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Universal gravitational mass">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tleft">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Universal_gravitational_mass.PNG">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="290" data-file-width="459" height="139" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Universal_gravitational_mass.PNG/220px-Universal_gravitational_mass.PNG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Universal_gravitational_mass.PNG/330px-Universal_gravitational_mass.PNG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/68/Universal_gravitational_mass.PNG/440px-Universal_gravitational_mass.PNG 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Universal_gravitational_mass.PNG" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         An apple experiences gravitational fields directed towards every part of the Earth; however, the sum total of these many fields produces a single gravitational field directed towards the Earth\'s center\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In contrast to earlier theories (e.g.\n       <a href="/wiki/Celestial_spheres" title="Celestial spheres">\n        celestial spheres\n       </a>\n       ) which stated that the heavens were made of entirely different material, Newton\'s theory of mass was groundbreaking partly because it introduced\n       <a href="/wiki/Newton%27s_law_of_universal_gravitation" title="Newton\'s law of universal gravitation">\n        universal gravitational mass\n       </a>\n       : every object has gravitational mass, and therefore, every object generates a gravitational field. Newton further assumed that the strength of each object\'s gravitational field would decrease according to the square of the distance to that object. If a large collection of small objects were formed into a giant spherical body such as the Earth or Sun, Newton calculated the collection would create a gravitational field proportional to the total mass of the body,\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-principia_22-2">\n        <a href="#cite_note-principia-22">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :397\n       </sup>\n       and inversely proportional to the square of the distance to the body\'s center.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-principia_22-3">\n        <a href="#cite_note-principia-22">\n         [17]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" style="white-space:nowrap;">\n        :221\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24">\n        <a href="#cite_note-24">\n         [note 6]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       For example, according to Newton\'s theory of universal gravitation, each carob seed produces a gravitational field. Therefore, if one were to gather an immense number of carob seeds and form them into an enormous sphere, then the gravitational field of the sphere would be proportional to the number of carob seeds in the sphere. Hence, it should be theoretically possible to determine the exact number of carob seeds that would be required to produce a gravitational field similar to that of the Earth or Sun. In fact, by\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Unit_conversion" title="Unit conversion">\n        unit conversion\n       </a>\n       it is a simple matter of abstraction to realize that any traditional mass unit can theoretically be used to measure gravitational mass.\n      </p>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Cavendish_Experiment.png">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="524" data-file-width="699" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Cavendish_Experiment.png/220px-Cavendish_Experiment.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Cavendish_Experiment.png/330px-Cavendish_Experiment.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Cavendish_Experiment.png/440px-Cavendish_Experiment.png 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Cavendish_Experiment.png" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Vertical section drawing of Cavendish\'s torsion balance instrument including the building in which it was housed. The large balls were hung from a frame so they could be rotated into position next to the small balls by a pulley from outside. Figure 1 of Cavendish\'s paper.\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Measuring gravitational mass in terms of traditional mass units is simple in principle, but extremely difficult in practice. According to Newton\'s theory all objects produce gravitational fields and it is theoretically possible to collect an immense number of small objects and form them into an enormous gravitating sphere. However, from a practical standpoint, the gravitational fields of small objects are extremely weak and difficult to measure. Newton\'s books on universal gravitation were published in the 1680s, but the first successful measurement of the Earth\'s mass in terms of traditional mass units, the\n       <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_experiment" title="Cavendish experiment">\n        Cavendish experiment\n       </a>\n       , did not occur until 1797, over a hundred years later. Cavendish found that the Earth\'s density was 5.448 \xc2\xb1 0.033 times that of water. As of 2009, the Earth\'s mass in kilograms is only known to around five digits of accuracy, whereas its gravitational mass is known to over nine significant figures.\n       <sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">\n        [\n        <i>\n         <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify">\n          <span title="&quot;mass in kilograms&quot; does not specify an experimental method. What\'s the distinction between the methods? (January 2014)">\n           clarification needed\n          </span>\n         </a>\n        </i>\n        ]\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Given two objects A and B, of masses\n       <i>\n        M\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        A\n       </sub>\n       and\n       <i>\n        M\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        B\n       </sub>\n       , separated by a\n       <a href="/wiki/Displacement_(vector)" title="Displacement (vector)">\n        displacement\n       </a>\n       <b>\n        R\n       </b>\n       <sub>\n        AB\n       </sub>\n       , Newton\'s law of gravitation states that each object exerts a gravitational force on the other, of magnitude\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow            F\n                </mi>\n               </mrow>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mtext>\n                 AB\n                </mtext>\n             <mi>\n               G\n              </mi>\n              <msub>\n               <mi>\n                M\n               </mi>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mtext>\n                 A\n                </mtext>\n               </mrow>\n              </msub>\n              <msub>\n               <mi>\n                M\n               </mi>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mtext>\n                 B\n                </mtext>\n               </mrow>\n              </msub>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <msub>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mover>\n                    <mrow                  ^\n                     <!-- ^ -->\n                    </mo>\n                   </mover>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </mrow>\n                 <mrow               <mtext>\n                    AB\n                   </mtext>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </msub>\n                 <msup>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mo stretchy="false">\n                    |\n                   </mo>\n                  </mrow>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mn>\n                    2\n                   </mn>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </msup>\n                </mrow>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mtext>\n              </mtext>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle \\mathbf {F} _{\\text{AB}}=-GM_{\\text{A}}M_{\\text{B}}{\\frac {{\\hat {\\mathbf {R} }}_{\\text{AB}}}{|\\mathbf {R} _{\\text{AB}}|^{2}}}\\ }\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="\\mathbf {F} _{\\text{AB}}=-GM_{\\text{A}}M_{\\text{B}}{\\frac {{\\hat {\\mathbf {R} }}_{\\text{AB}}}{|\\mathbf {R} _{\\text{AB}}|^{2}}}\\ " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/7db43c113a497c1d53ea7464344e17480d733d62" style="vertical-align: -3.171ex; width:26.955ex; height:7.343ex;"/>\n        </span>\n        ,\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       where\n       <i>\n        G\n       </i>\n       is the universal\n       <a href="/wiki/Gravitational_constant" title="Gravitational constant">\n        gravitational constant\n       </a>\n       . The above statement may be reformulated in the following way: if\n       <i>\n        g\n       </i>\n       is the magnitude at a given location in a gravitational field, then the gravitational force on an object with gravitational mass\n       <i>\n        M\n       </i>\n       is\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mi>\n               F\n              </mi>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mi>\n               M\n              </mi>\n              <mi>\n               g\n              </mi>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle F=Mg}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="F=Mg" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/433afb3fc3ba9017e5db2eab0616be1a526b4c3a" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.439ex; height:2.509ex;"/>\n        </span>\n        .\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       This is the basis by which masses are determined by\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Weighing" title="Weighing">\n        weighing\n       </a>\n       . In simple\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Spring_scales" title="Spring scales">\n        spring scales\n       </a>\n       , for example, the force\n       <i>\n        F\n       </i>\n       is proportional to the displacement of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Spring_(device)" title="Spring (device)">\n        spring\n       </a>\n       beneath the weighing pan, as per\n       <a href="/wiki/Hooke%27s_law" title="Hooke\'s law">\n        Hooke\'s law\n       </a>\n       , and the scales are\n       <a href="/wiki/Calibration" title="Calibration">\n        calibrated\n       </a>\n       to take\n       <i>\n        g\n       </i>\n       into account, allowing the mass\n       <i>\n        M\n       </i>\n       to be read off. Assuming the gravitational field is equivalent on both sides of the balance, a\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Beam_balance" title="Beam balance">\n        balance\n       </a>\n       measures relative weight, giving the relative gravitation mass of each object.\n      </p>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Inertial_mass">\n        Inertial mass\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=13" title="Edit section: Inertial mass">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="thumb tright">\n       <div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;">\n        <a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Massmeter.jpg">\n         <img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1500" data-file-width="1125" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Massmeter.jpg/220px-Massmeter.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Massmeter.jpg/330px-Massmeter.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2a/Massmeter.jpg/440px-Massmeter.jpg 2x" width="220"/>\n        </a>\n        <div class="thumbcaption">\n         <div class="magnify">\n          <a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Massmeter.jpg" title="Enlarge">\n          </a>\n         </div>\n         Massmeter, a device for measuring the inertial mass of an astronaut in weightlessness. The mass is calculated via the oscillation period for a spring with the astronaut attached (\n         <a href="/wiki/Tsiolkovsky_State_Museum_of_the_History_of_Cosmonautics" title="Tsiolkovsky State Museum of the History of Cosmonautics">\n          Tsiolkovsky State Museum of the History of Cosmonautics\n         </a>\n         )\n        </div>\n       </div>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       <i>\n        Inertial mass\n       </i>\n       is the mass of an object measured by its resistance to acceleration. This definition has been championed by\n       <a href="/wiki/Ernst_Mach" title="Ernst Mach">\n        Ernst Mach\n       </a>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25">\n        <a href="#cite_note-25">\n         [19]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-belkind_26-0">\n        <a href="#cite_note-belkind-26">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and has since been developed into the notion of\n       <a href="/wiki/Operationalization" title="Operationalization">\n        operationalism\n       </a>\n       by\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Percy_W._Bridgman" title="Percy W. Bridgman">\n        Percy W. Bridgman\n       </a>\n       .\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27">\n        <a href="#cite_note-27">\n         [21]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28">\n        <a href="#cite_note-28">\n         [22]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       The simple\n       <a href="/wiki/Classical_mechanics" title="Classical mechanics">\n        classical mechanics\n       </a>\n       definition of mass is slightly different than the definition in the theory of\n       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">\n        special relativity\n       </a>\n       , but the essential meaning is the same.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       In classical mechanics, according to\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_second_law" title="Newton\'s second law">\n        Newton\'s second law\n       </a>\n       , we say that a body has a mass\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       if, at any instant of time, it obeys the equation of motion\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                F\n               </mi>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mi>\n               m\n              </mi>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                a\n               </mi>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               ,\n              </mo>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle \\mathbf {F} =m\\mathbf {a} ,}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="\\mathbf {F} =m\\mathbf {a} ," aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/93aa54e6c7e8df66d85d06b6eb0b0a2d3ec4ce20" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.82ex; height:2.509ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       where\n       <b>\n        F\n       </b>\n       is the resultant\n       <a href="/wiki/Force" title="Force">\n        force\n       </a>\n       acting on the body and\n       <b>\n        a\n       </b>\n       is the\n       <a href="/wiki/Acceleration" title="Acceleration">\n        acceleration\n       </a>\n       of the body\'s centre of mass.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29">\n        <a href="#cite_note-29">\n         [note 7]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       For the moment, we will put aside the question of what "force acting on the body" actually means.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       This equation illustrates how mass relates to the\n       <a href="/wiki/Inertia" title="Inertia">\n        inertia\n       </a>\n       of a body. Consider two objects with different masses. If we apply an identical force to each, the object with a bigger mass will experience a smaller acceleration, and the object with a smaller mass will experience a bigger acceleration. We might say that the larger mass exerts a greater "resistance" to changing its state of motion in response to the force.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       However, this notion of applying "identical" forces to different objects brings us back to the fact that we have not really defined what a force is. We can sidestep this difficulty with the help of\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Newton%27s_third_law" title="Newton\'s third law">\n        Newton\'s third law\n       </a>\n       , which states that if one object exerts a force on a second object, it will experience an equal and opposite force. To be precise, suppose we have two objects of constant inertial masses\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       and\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       . We isolate the two objects from all other physical influences, so that the only forces present are the force exerted on\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       by\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       , which we denote\n       <b>\n        F\n       </b>\n       <sub>\n        12\n       </sub>\n       , and the force exerted on\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       by\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       , which we denote\n       <b>\n        F\n       </b>\n       <sub>\n        21\n       </sub>\n       . Newton\'s second law states that\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mtable columnalign="right left right left right left right left right left right left" columnspacing="0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em 2em 0em" displaystyle="true" rowspacing="3pt">\n                <mtr>\n                 <mtd>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <msub>\n                    <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                     F\n                    </mi>\n                    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                     <mn mathvariant="bold">\n                      12\n                     </mn>\n                    </mrow>\n                   </msub>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </mtd>\n                 <mtd>\n                  <mi>\n                  </mi>\n                  <mo>\n                   =\n                  </mo>\n                  <msub>\n                   <mi>\n                    m\n                   </mi>\n                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                    <mn>\n                     1\n                    </mn>\n                   </mrow>\n                  </msub>\n                  <msub>\n                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                    <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                     a\n                    </mi>\n                   </mrow>\n                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                    <mn>\n                     1\n                    </mn>\n                   </mrow>\n                  </msub>\n                  <mo>\n                   ,\n                  </mo>\n                 </mtd>\n                </mtr>\n                <mtr>\n                 <mtd>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <msub>\n                    <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                     F\n                    </mi>\n                    <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                     <mn mathvariant="bold">\n                      21\n                     </mn>\n                    </mrow>\n                   </msub>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </mtd>\n                 <mtd>\n                  <mi>\n                  </mi>\n                  <mo>\n                   =\n                  </mo>\n                  <msub>\n                   <mi>\n                    m\n                   </mi>\n                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                    <mn>\n                     2\n                    </mn>\n                   </mrow>\n                  </msub>\n                  <msub>\n                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                    <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                     a\n                    </mi>\n                   </mrow>\n                   <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                    <mn>\n                     2\n                    </mn>\n                   </mrow>\n                  </msub>\n                  <mo>\n                   ,\n                  </mo>\n                 </mtd>\n                </mtr>\n               </mtable>\n              </mrow>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle {\\begin{aligned}\\mathbf {F_{12}} &amp;=m_{1}\\mathbf {a} _{1},\\\\\\mathbf {F_{21}} &amp;=m_{2}\\mathbf {a} _{2},\\end{aligned}}}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="{\\begin{aligned}\\mathbf {F_{12}} &amp;=m_{1}\\mathbf {a} _{1},\\\\\\mathbf {F_{21}} &amp;=m_{2}\\mathbf {a} _{2},\\end{aligned}}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/b35bcddbcf7727e7f3e30bb49f5bb03d2d07dac7" style="vertical-align: -2.338ex; width:13.832ex; height:5.843ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       where\n       <b>\n        a\n       </b>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       and\n       <b>\n        a\n       </b>\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       are the accelerations of\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       and\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       , respectively. Suppose that these accelerations are non-zero, so that the forces between the two objects are non-zero. This occurs, for example, if the two objects are in the process of colliding with one another. Newton\'s third law then states that\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display:           <msub>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                 F\n                </mi>\n               </mrow>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>\n                 12\n                </mn>\n               </mrow>\n              </msub>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mo>\n               \xe2\x88\x92\n               <!-- \xe2\x88\x92 -->\n              </mo>\n              <msub>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                 F\n                </mi>\n               </mrow>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>\n                 21\n                </mn>\n               </mrow>\n              </msub>\n              <mo>\n               ;\n              </mo>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle \\mathbf {F} _{12}=-\\mathbf {F} _{21};}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="\\mathbf {F} _{12}=-\\mathbf {F} _{21};" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a5cdfe6065d9d333568f83e41c49f4b7f491caad" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:12.753ex; height:2.509ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       and thus\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <msub>\n               <mi>\n                m\n               </mi>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>\n                 1\n                </mn>\n               </mrow>\n              </msub>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <msub>\n               <mi>\n                m\n               </mi>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>\n                 2\n                </mn>\n               </mrow>\n              </msub>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <mrow>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mo stretchy="false">\n                   |\n                  </mo>\n                 </mrow>\n                 <msub>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                    a\n                   </mi>\n                  </mrow>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mn>\n                    2\n                   </mn>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </msub>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mo stretchy="false">\n                   |\n                  </mo>\n                 </mrow>\n                </mrow>\n                <mrow>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mo stretchy="false">\n                   |\n                  </mo>\n                 </mrow>\n                 <msub>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                    a\n                   </mi>\n                  </mrow>\n                  <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                   <mn>\n                    1\n                   </mn>\n                  </mrow>\n                 </msub>\n                 <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                  <mo stretchy="false">\n                   |\n                  </mo>\n                 </mrow>\n                </mrow>\n               </mfrac>\n              </mrow>\n              <mspace width="negativethinmathspace">\n              </mspace>\n              <mo>\n               .\n              </mo>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle m_{1}=m_{2}{\\frac {|\\mathbf {a} _{2}|}{|\\mathbf {a} _{1}|}}\\!.}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="m_{1}=m_{2}{\\frac {|\\mathbf {a} _{2}|}{|\\mathbf {a} _{1}|}}\\!." aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/5ffc3f2a489f575b7916027648b1db6e44e82ad1" style="vertical-align: -2.671ex; width:14.126ex; height:6.509ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       If |\n       <b>\n        a\n       </b>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       | is non-zero, the fraction is well-defined, which allows us to measure the inertial mass of\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       . In this case,\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       is our "reference" object, and we can define its mass\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       as (say) 1\xc2\xa0kilogram. Then we can measure the mass of any other object in the universe by colliding it with the reference object and measuring the accelerations.\n      </p>\n      <p>\n       Additionally, mass relates a body\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Momentum" title="Momentum">\n        momentum\n       </a>\n       <b>\n        p\n       </b>\n       to its linear\n       <a href="/wiki/Velocity" title="Velocity">\n        velocity\n       </a>\n       <b>\n        v\n       </b>\n       :\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                p\n               </mi>\n              </mrow>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mi>\n               m\n              </mi>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                v\n               </mi>\n              </mrow>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle \\mathbf {p} =m\\mathbf {v} }\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="\\mathbf {p} =m\\mathbf {v} " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a271a96e7b925fd39686375167c76d406e87c813" style="vertical-align: -0.671ex; width:8.077ex; height:2.009ex;"/>\n        </span>\n        ,\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       and the body\'s\n       <a href="/wiki/Kinetic_energy" title="Kinetic energy">\n        kinetic energy\n       </a>\n       <i>\n        K\n       </i>\n       to its velocity:\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mi>\n               K\n              </mi>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n                <mfrac>\n                 <mn>\n                  1\n                 </mn>\n                 <mn>\n                  2\n                 </mn>\n                </mfrac>\n               </mstyle>\n              </mrow>\n              <mi>\n               m\n              </mi>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mo stretchy="false">\n                |\n               </mo>\n              </mrow>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mi mathvariant="bold">\n                v\n               </mi>\n              </mrow>\n              <msup>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mo stretchy="false">\n                 |\n                </mo>\n               </mrow>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mn>\n                 2\n                </mn>\n               </mrow>\n              </msup>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle K={\\dfrac {1}{2}}m|\\mathbf {v} |^{2}}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="K={\\dfrac {1}{2}}m|\\mathbf {v} |^{2}" aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/e9db5c9649c22a633b7f7e36154be51bad32d9c1" style="vertical-align: -1.838ex; width:13.043ex; height:5.176ex;"/>\n        </span>\n        .\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       The primary difficulty with Mach\'s definition of mass is that it fails to take into account the\n       <a href="/wiki/Potential_energy" title="Potential energy">\n        potential energy\n       </a>\n       (or\n       <a href="/wiki/Binding_energy" title="Binding energy">\n        binding energy\n       </a>\n       ) needed to bring two masses sufficiently close to one another to perform the measurement of mass.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-belkind_26-1">\n        <a href="#cite_note-belkind-26">\n         [20]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       This is most vividly demonstrated by comparing the mass of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Proton" title="Proton">\n        proton\n       </a>\n       in the nucleus of\n       <a href="/wiki/Deuterium" title="Deuterium">\n        deuterium\n       </a>\n       , to the mass of the proton in free space (which is greater by about 0.239% - this is due to the binding energy of deuterium.). Thus, for example, if the reference weight\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       is taken to be the mass of the neutron in free space, and the relative accelerations for the proton and neutron in deuterium are computed, then the above formula over-estimates the mass\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       (by 0.239%) for the proton in deuterium. At best, Mach\'s formula can only be used to obtain ratios of masses, that is, as\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       /\n       <i>\n        m\n       </i>\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       = |\n       <b>\n        a\n       </b>\n       <sub>\n        2\n       </sub>\n       | / |\n       <b>\n        a\n       </b>\n       <sub>\n        1\n       </sub>\n       |. An additional difficulty was pointed out by\n       <a href="/wiki/Henri_Poincar%C3%A9" title="Henri Poincar\xc3\xa9">\n        Henri Poincar\xc3\xa9\n       </a>\n       , which is that the measurement of instantaneous acceleration is impossible: unlike the measurement of time or distance, there is no way to measure acceleration with a single measurement; one must make multiple measurements (of position, time, etc.) and perform a computation to obtain the acceleration. Poincar\xc3\xa9 termed this to be an "insurmountable flaw" in the Mach definition of mass.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30">\n        <a href="#cite_note-30">\n         [23]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Atomic_mass">\n        Atomic mass\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=14" title="Edit section: Atomic mass">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">\n        Atomic mass unit\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       Typically, the mass of objects is measured in relation to that of the kilogram, which is defined as the mass of the\n       <a href="/wiki/Kilogram#International_prototype_kilogram" title="Kilogram">\n        <i>\n         international prototype kilogram\n        </i>\n       </a>\n       (IPK), a platinum alloy cylinder stored in an environmentally-monitored safe secured in a vault at the\n       <a href="/wiki/International_Bureau_of_Weights_and_Measures" title="International Bureau of Weights and Measures">\n        International Bureau of Weights and Measures\n       </a>\n       in France. However, the IPK is not convenient for measuring the masses of atoms and particles of similar scale, as it contains trillions of trillions of atoms, and has most certainly lost or gained a little mass over time despite the best efforts to prevent this. It is much easier to precisely compare an atom\'s mass to that of another atom, thus scientists developed the\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Atomic_mass_unit" title="Atomic mass unit">\n        atomic mass unit\n       </a>\n       (or Dalton). By definition, 1\xc2\xa0u is exactly one twelfth of the mass of a\n       <a href="/wiki/Carbon-12" title="Carbon-12">\n        carbon-12\n       </a>\n       atom, and by extension a carbon-12 atom has a mass of exactly 12\xc2\xa0u. This definition, however, might be changed by the\n       <a href="/wiki/Proposed_redefinition_of_SI_base_units" title="Proposed redefinition of SI base units">\n        proposed redefinition of SI base units\n       </a>\n       , which will leave the Dalton very close to one, but no longer exactly equal to it.\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31">\n        <a href="#cite_note-31">\n         [24]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32">\n        <a href="#cite_note-32">\n         [25]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n      </p>\n      <h2>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Mass_in_relativity">\n        Mass in relativity\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=15" title="Edit section: Mass in relativity">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h2>\n      <h3>\n       <span class="mw-headline" id="Special_relativity">\n        Special relativity\n       </span>\n       <span class="mw-editsection">\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         [\n        </span>\n        <a href="/w/index.php?title=Mass&amp;action=edit&amp;section=16" title="Edit section: Special relativity">\n         edit\n        </a>\n        <span class="mw-editsection-bracket">\n         ]\n        </span>\n       </span>\n      </h3>\n      <div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">\n       Main article:\n       <a href="/wiki/Mass_in_special_relativity" title="Mass in special relativity">\n        Mass in special relativity\n       </a>\n      </div>\n      <p>\n       In\n       <a href="/wiki/Special_relativity" title="Special relativity">\n        special relativity\n       </a>\n       , there are two kinds of mass:\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rest_mass" title="Rest mass">\n        rest mass\n       </a>\n       (invariant mass),\n       <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33">\n        <a href="#cite_note-33">\n         [note 8]\n        </a>\n       </sup>\n       and\n       <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Relativistic_mass" title="Relativistic mass">\n        relativistic mass\n       </a>\n       (which increases with velocity). Rest mass is the Newtonian mass as measured by an observer moving along with the object.\n       <i>\n        Relativistic mass\n       </i>\n       is the total quantity of energy in a body or system divided by\n       <a href="/wiki/Speed_of_light" title="Speed of light">\n        <i>\n         c\n        </i>\n       </a>\n       <sup>\n        2\n       </sup>\n       . The two are related by the following equation:\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" scriptlevel="0">\n              <msub>\n               <mi>\n                m\n               </mi>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  r\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  e\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  l\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  a\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  t\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  i\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  v\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  e\n                 </mi>\n                </mrow>\n               </mrow>\n              </msub>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mi>\n               \xce\xb3\n               <!-- \xce\xb3 -->\n              </mi>\n              <mo stretchy="false">\n               (\n              </mo>\n              <msub>\n               <mi>\n                m\n               </mi>\n               <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  r\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  e\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  s\n                 </mi>\n                 <mi mathvariant="normal">\n                  t\n                 </mi>\n                </mrow>\n               </mrow>\n              </msub>\n              <mo stretchy="false">\n               )\n              </mo>\n              <mspace width="negativethinmathspace">\n              </mspace>\n             </mstyle>\n            </mrow>\n            <annotation encoding="application/x-tex">\n             {\\displaystyle m_{\\mathrm {relative} }=\\gamma (m_{\\mathrm {rest} })\\!}\n            </annotation>\n           </semantics>\n          </math>\n         </span>\n         <img alt="m_{\\mathrm {relative} }=\\gamma (m_{\\mathrm {rest} })\\!" margin-right: -0.166ex; width:18.657ex; height:2.843ex;"/>\n        </span>\n       </dd>\n      </dl>\n      <p>\n       where\n       <span class="mwe-math-element">\n        <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n         <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n          <semantics>\n           <mrow </span>\n        <img alt="\\gamma " aria-hidden="true" class="mwe-math-fallback-image-inline" src="https://wikimedia.org/api/rest_v1/media/math/render/svg/a223c880b0ce3da8f64ee33c4f0010beee400b1a"      :\n      </p>\n      <dl>\n       <dd>\n        <span class="mwe-math-element">\n         <span class="mwe-math-mathml-inline mwe-math-mathml-a11y" style="display: none;">\n          <math xmlns="http://www.w3.org/1998/Math/MathML">\n           <semantics>\n            <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n             <mstyle displaystyle="true" scriptlevel="0">\n              <mi>\n               \xce\xb3\n               <!-- \xce\xb3 -->\n              </mi>\n              <mo>\n               =\n              </mo>\n              <mrow class="MJX-TeXAtom-ORD">\n               <mfrac>\n                <mn>\n                 1\n                </mn>\n                <msqrt>\n                 <mn>\n                  1\n                 </mn>\n                 <mo>\n                  \xe2\x88\x92\n                  <!-- \xe2\x88\x92 -->\n                 </mo>\n                 <msup>\n                  <mi>\n                   v\n                  </mi>\n                  <mrow
